  <div class="friendReviews elementListBrown" >
    <div class="section">
      

<div id="review_1759746222" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1759746222" />
    <a title="stormin" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/3809399-stormin"><img alt="stormin" src="https://s.gr-assets.com/assets/nophoto/user/m_50x66-82093808bca726cb3249a493fbd3bd0f.png" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1759746222?book_show_action=true">Sep 16, 2016</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="stormin" class="user" itemprop="url" name="stormin" href="/user/show/3809399-stormin">stormin</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/3809399-stormin?shelf=sci-fi">sci-fi</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1759746222" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer10420398966577274630">First, I love the title. "Orphans of the Sky," is one of those titles that makes me love sci-fi. <br/><br/>Second, this is one of those books that originated--or at least was an early adopter--of ideas that have come to be central to sci-fi. In this case, the concept is a generation ship. Generation ships are slow-moving space ships that would take so long to get from point A to point B that entire generations would live and die in the ship during the journey (hence the name). According to Wikipedia, the </span>
  <span id="freeText10420398966577274630" style="display:none">First, I love the title. "Orphans of the Sky," is one of those titles that makes me love sci-fi. <br /><br />Second, this is one of those books that originated--or at least was an early adopter--of ideas that have come to be central to sci-fi. In this case, the concept is a generation ship. Generation ships are slow-moving space ships that would take so long to get from point A to point B that entire generations would live and die in the ship during the journey (hence the name). According to Wikipedia, the initial versions of the story (originally published in 1941) were "one of the earliest fictional depictions of a generation ship."<br /><br />It's especially interesting that right along with an early depiction of a generation ship you get the early depiction of one particular way they can go wrong: nobody on the ship remembers that they're even on a ship. This is one of the oldest and most productive ideas in sci-fi, and it's still being used in stories today. <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/author/show/3064305.Hugh_Howey" title="Hugh Howey" rel="nofollow noopener">Hugh Howey</a>'s <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/17817659.Wool" title="Wool by Hugh Howey" rel="nofollow noopener">Wool</a>, for example, has basically the identical concept: a bunch of people living in a sealed environment for so long that they have forgotten why they came to be there or the nature of the world outside. Along the way, they've developed their own culture and beliefs. Of course, in Wool the people are actually in underground silos on Earth instead of on a spaceship, but it's still essentially the same idea: an enclosed community lasting multiple generations.<br /><br />I guess I shouldn't be surprised, though. The first time I heard of generation ships--back when I was in middle school--practically my very first story idea was identical to this one: a kid growing up in a space ship traveling between the stars where no one actually knows that they're inside a space ship. (Someone even told me the idea had been done before when I talked about it with adults I knew, but I didn't get around to reading the book they mentioned--Orphans of the Sky--for another 20 years.)<br /><br />There's another major theme that this book tackles: science vs. religion. Once you've got a bunch of people living on a spaceship where gravity is created by spinning the ship in a circle, it's easy to create scientists who come up with rational explanations for things that make absolutely no sense compared to our understanding of physics. So Robert Heinlein dedicates quite a lot of space to depicting "scientists" (as they're known on the ship) as basically the exact copy of your stereotypical scholastic religious zealot. They have some physics textbooks, but the physics described in them--especially gravity--makes no sense to people who know that your weight increases or decreases as you climb up or down levels in the ship. As a result, they refuse to interpret the physics textbooks literally and instead treat them as mystical or metaphorical. <br /><br />It's funny, because the points that Heinlein is making--which are primarily about the way that being open- or close-minded is independent of being theist or atheist--seems equally applicable in 2016 as it did when the stories were first published in 1941.<br /><br />These are the reasons I think the book is worth reading: it's an absolutely vital component of our early sci-fi canon.<br /><br />But--other than as an interesting view into sci-fi history--I can't really recommend it. It's light and fluffy enough--written with Heinlein's distinctive "Let me explain how the world works, young person" tone--but the story doesn't really make a lot of sense (especially the ending) and the characters are all drawn in crayon. It's a simplistic book, and I think that makes sense, because when sci-fi was still fresh and new coming up with the idea of a generation ship (or a story to put one in) <em>at all</em> was the accomplishment. There's no reason to be too harsh, I think, as long as you approach the book as a 1940s-era YA novel (which I think is what it was, although I'm not sure about the demographics of Astounding Science Fiction back then.)<br /><br />Lastly, I have to say a word about the women in this book: I have never read such a casually misogynistic book in my life. I <em>think</em>--although this is my guess based on reading a fairly large volume of Heinlein over the years--that the point he was trying to make is that in less-developed societies women were mistreated because the society placed such a premium on physical prowess. The idea being that in a technologically sophisticated society, there was room for greater appreciation of the finer things in life, which includes femininity. So, when the protagonist casually beats his second wife (at the same time, polygamy is a thing) to force her into doing whatever he says, the point is not that Heinlein approves, I think, but rather that that kind of barbarism comes hand-in-hand with a regression in science. The idea is that morality and technology are intertwined, which sort of shifts the emphasis away from sexism and towards anti-multi-culturalism. <br /><br />This kind of thing is why Heinlein is such a lightning rod even today, by the way. He's one of those divisive figures long after his death. If you declare that you're still an unqualified Heinlein fan, that marks you out as being a libertarian, conservative, or at least iconoclast. If you're going to be a socially conscious sci-fi fan, then you have to at least temper your admiration of him, treating him as kind of like a racist elderly relative. And that's the best case scenario.<br /><br />Me?<br /><br />I have a mixed opinion of Heinlein too, but for different reasons. His stories often don't make a lot of sense on closer inspection, but they are told with such utter, unself-conscious <em>conviction</em> that--if you let them--they will take you along for a fun ride. I have to admit I'm also a little jealous, because I feel like writing sci-fi was a lot easier in 1940 when all these ideas were fresh and new and nobody had done them before. <br /><br />But, politically, I think Heinlein deserves a lot of slack. As I said in my review of <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/50840.Farnham_s_Freehold" title="Farnham's Freehold by Robert A. Heinlein" rel="nofollow noopener">Farnham's Freehold</a>, Heinlein's <em>intentions</em> were clearly towards equality. He was contemptuous of multi-culturalism, and I'm pretty sure he would have stood by my characterization that any society that wasn't technological and literate was barbaric and inferior. However, he was also clearly dedicated to the idea that all individuals are equal. If you are born and raised in an illiterate society, you're going to be barbaric, but (1) that's true of anyone of any race and (2) even barbaric societies have bravery and cleverness, but these characteristics have to be defined contextually. <br /><br />When it came to gender, I think he had very definite ideas that men and women are intrinsically different and so--again--he's going to violate 21st century social norms pretty brutally. But it's worth pointing out that he often depicted very smart, very independent women (frequently with a lot more talent than the men around them) and even directly confronted the insidious effects of chauvinism and discrimination on those women (I'm thinking of <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/50848.The_Rolling_Stones" title="The Rolling Stones by Robert A. Heinlein" rel="nofollow noopener">The Rolling Stones</a> for this one). <br /><br />In other words: I think Heinlein probably got a lot of stuff wrong, but I think he deserves credit for earnestly holding to ideals of equality and fairness as he saw them. The commitment to those principles matters more, I think, than necessarily having the perfect explanation of how they should be implemented in society.<br /><br />Anyways, that's a bit of a digression, but hopefully an interesting one.<br /><br />The short version: this is vintage Heinlein and well worth reading as a window into early sci-fi. Thematically, it's as fresh today as it was when it was written. But in terms of actual enjoyment, you'd do better with a more recent take. This book is primarily of historical interest.</span>
  <a data-text-id="10420398966577274630" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

  <span id="freeText16826342254134483352" style="display:none">I've held off on writing this review for so many reasons. This was an Audible offering and I was excited to return to an author of my youth. Today, I'm sad for my young female teen self that had novels like this to guide her future. Yes, women are used to reading male main characters without question or concern. However this great story dives hard into female prejudices that should have shocked me even in the late 1960's and early 1970's. <br /><br />I simply cannot recommend it unless it is a woman's literature historical perspective. yes, that is a tiny reading group.<br /><br />Again, I wonder how I thought his books were so wonderful as I read them in high school. No wonder I loved Madeleine L'Engle and I had her books to read. <br /><br />There will be no more Robert Heinlein books in my reading list. Enough is enough.<br /><br />I so want to hug that teenage girl and wish I could tell her that society was wrong about women. We are capable, brave, heroic, and wonderful role models.<br /><br />This is further wrapped up in feelings that I will relive as I head to an FBI writer's seminar in NYC. As me about my visit to the Washington DC headquarter's building as a teenager. NO WONDER THERE ARE A BUNCH OF ANGRY 50's WOMEN OUT THERE. I'm amazed we aren't more messed up then we are.</span>

  <span id="freeText13740043113087278808" style="display:none">review coming... was good up until the hero took two women and beat them up when they didn't do as they were told or when he was displeased by them. :/<br /><br />So, god damn it, Sci-Fi was most certainly not for girls back in the 50's and 60's. <br /><br />I liked the idea of a ship that kept building layers and layers ontop of itself and that the original 'explorers' and their 'mission' became lost over time. <div id="review_1662281028" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1662281028" />
    <a title="Joseph" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/5004971-joseph"><img alt="Joseph" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1523632271p2/5004971.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1662281028?book_show_action=true">Jun 07, 2016</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Joseph" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Joseph" href="/user/show/5004971-joseph">Joseph</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Kindle Edition " href="/book/show/10501035-orphans-of-the-sky">review of another edition</a>


        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1662281028" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer11679085950635483790">I don't know if this was actually the <i>first</i> generation starship story, but it was almost certainly the most influential. Hugh Hoyston was born (as were his to-the-nth-generation ancestors) on the Ship, but to him it's just the world -- it's only natural that the Decks curve and that your weight decreases as you ascend. Then he gets captured by the Muties (Mutineers/Mutants -- take your pick) and their two-headed leader Joe-Jim, and discovers that there's actually an <i>outside</i> to the world ...<br/><br/>This </span>
  <span id="freeText11679085950635483790" style="display:none">I don't know if this was actually the <i>first</i> generation starship story, but it was almost certainly the most influential. Hugh Hoyston was born (as were his to-the-nth-generation ancestors) on the Ship, but to him it's just the world -- it's only natural that the Decks curve and that your weight decreases as you ascend. Then he gets captured by the Muties (Mutineers/Mutants -- take your pick) and their two-headed leader Joe-Jim, and discovers that there's actually an <i>outside</i> to the world ...<br /><br />This is relatively early Heinlein (1941) and short (two linked ... novelettes, maybe?); but one thing it demonstrates beyond question is that Heinlein could <i>write</i>. The prose is economical but evocative and goes down smoothly. The characters are, for the most part, engaging, although the less said about the women (about whom, in point of fact, he wrote almost nothing in this story) the better.</span>
  <a data-text-id="11679085950635483790" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_2167636053" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2167636053" />
    <a title="Ericka Clouther" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/1947301-ericka-clouther"><img alt="Ericka Clouther" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1550700903p2/1947301.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2167636053?book_show_action=true">Nov 02, 2017</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Ericka Clouther" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Ericka Clouther" href="/user/show/1947301-ericka-clouther">Ericka Clouther</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1947301-ericka-clouther?shelf=scifi">scifi</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1947301-ericka-clouther?shelf=1960s">1960s</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1947301-ericka-clouther?shelf=most-read-author">most-read-author</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1947301-ericka-clouther?shelf=book-club-dad">book-club-dad</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1947301-ericka-clouther?shelf=0-own">0-own</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1947301-ericka-clouther?shelf=own-fiction">own-fiction</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1947301-ericka-clouther?shelf=own-read">own-read</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1947301-ericka-clouther?shelf=fiction">fiction</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1947301-ericka-clouther?shelf=author-male">author-male</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1947301-ericka-clouther?shelf=1940s">1940s</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2167636053" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer902274602613213721">Fun idea for science fiction and interesting religious metaphor. People aboard a generational ship forget everything about Earth, science, and technology, and as the areas with windows to the outside are shut off, they forget even that they are on a ship, but begin to think the ship is all of existence. It's a brilliant concept and I would have loved to see how StarTrek (STNG) would have handled it. Unfortunately, Heinlein's version is not very deeply thoughtful. The characters are not developed</span>
  <span id="freeText902274602613213721" style="display:none">Fun idea for science fiction and interesting religious metaphor. People aboard a generational ship forget everything about Earth, science, and technology, and as the areas with windows to the outside are shut off, they forget even that they are on a ship, but begin to think the ship is all of existence. It's a brilliant concept and I would have loved to see how StarTrek (STNG) would have handled it. Unfortunately, Heinlein's version is not very deeply thoughtful. The characters are not developed very deeply, the society is very primitive and extremely stubborn, and most disappointingly, the women never get revenge for all the sexism inflicted on them. The mutated humans are a fun twist, especially Siamese twins Joe and Jim. While Joe-Jim is not treated with a lot of dignity by other members of the crew, they are accorded a fair amount of respect by Heinlein, at least relative to the other characters.</span>
  <a data-text-id="902274602613213721" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

  function submitShelfLink(unique_id, book_id, shelf_id, shelf_name, submit_form, exclusive) {
    var checkbox_id = 'shelf_name_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    var element = document.getElementById(checkbox_id)

    var checked = element.checked
    if (checked && exclusive) {
      // can't uncheck a radio by clicking it!
      return
    }
    if(document.getElementById("savingMessage")){
      Element.show('savingMessage')
    }
    var element_id = 'shelfInDropdownName_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    Element.update(element_id, "saving...");
    if (submit_form) {
      Element.hide('shelfDropdown_' + unique_id)
      var form = document.getElementById('addBookForm' + book_id)
      if (form) {
        form.shelf.value = shelf_name
        form.onsubmit()
      }
    }
    else {
      var action = checked ? 'remove' : ''
      element.checked = !element.checked
      new Ajax.Request('/shelf/add_to_shelf', {asynchronous:true, evalScripts:true, onSuccess:function(request){shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name)}, parameters:'book_id=' + book_id + '&name=' + shelf_name + '&a=' + action + '&authenticity_token=' + encodeURIComponent('O0PizEegHn9d1QxyxGY35CV9dFAaoQt+sqKdJp6ZlO61ADIq8rHjzCt25duUMcd01qBQRBM18q3f/fKcuZBHBg==')})
    }
  }

  function shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name) {
    Element.update('shelfListfalse_' + book_id, request.responseText)
    afterShelfSave(checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name.escapeHTML())
  }

  function refreshGroupBox(group_id, book_id) {
    new Ajax.Updater('addGroupBooks' + book_id + '', '/group/add_book_box', {asynchronous:true, evalScripts:true, onSuccess:function(request){refreshGroupBoxComplete(request, book_id);}, parameters:'id=' + group_id + '&book_id=' + book_id + '&refresh=true' + '&authenticity_token=' + encodeURIComponent('jzHpq8eHRQupAvIjWJ87qaSzGWKYEiFQZmilJgI/D+oBcjlNcpa4uN+hG4oIyMs5V249dpGG2IMLN8qcJTbcAg==')})
  }

<div class='bookAuthorProfile__about'>

<span id="freeTextContainer14027149300634445520">Robert Anson Heinlein was an American novelist and science fiction writer. Often called "the dean of science fiction writers", he is one of the most popular, influential, and controversial authors of "hard science fiction". <br/><br/>He set a high standard for science and engineering plausibility and helped to raise the genre's standards of literary quality. He was the first SF writer to break into mainstre</span>
  <span id="freeText14027149300634445520" style="display:none">Robert Anson Heinlein was an American novelist and science fiction writer. Often called "the dean of science fiction writers", he is one of the most popular, influential, and controversial authors of "hard science fiction". <br /><br />He set a high standard for science and engineering plausibility and helped to raise the genre's standards of literary quality. He was the first SF writer to break into mainstream, general magazines such as <i>The Saturday Evening Post</i>, in the late 1940s. He was also among the first authors of bestselling, novel-length science fiction in the modern, mass-market era. <br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="14027149300634445520" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

      <div class=" clearFloats bigBox"><div class="h2Container gradientHeaderContainer"><h2 class="brownBackground"><a href="/work/quotes/1021849-orphans-of-the-sky">Quotes from Orphans of the Sky</a></h2></div><div class="bigBoxBody"><div class="bigBoxContent containerWithHeaderContent">
          <div class="stacked" tyle="padding-bottom:15px">
            <span class="readable">&ldquo;Two bodies attract each other directly as the product of their masses and inversely as the square of their distance.' It sounds like a rule for simple physical facts, does it not? Yet it is nothing of the sort; it was the poetical way the old ones had of expressing the rule of propinquity which governs the emotion of love. The bodies referred to are human bodies, mass is their capacity for love. Young people have a greater capacity for love than the elderly; when thy are thrown together they fall in love, yet when they are separated they soon get over it. 'Out of sight, out of mind.' It's as simple as that. But you were seeking some deep meaning for it.&rdquo;</span>
            <nobr>
              &mdash;
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/quotes/102976-two-bodies-attract-each-other-directly-as-the-product-of">26 likes</a>
            </nobr>
            <div class="clear"></div>
          </div>
          <div class="stacked" tyle="padding-bottom:15px">
            <span class="readable">&ldquo;There is a misconception, geocentric and anthropomorphic, common to the large majority the the earthbound, which causes them to visualize a planetary system stereoscopically. The mind's eye sees a sun, remote from a backdrop of stars, and surrounded by spinning apples -- the planets. Step out on your balcony and look. Can you tell the planets from the stars? Venus you may pick out with ease, but could you tell it from Canopus, if you had not previously been introduced? That little red speck -- is it Mars, or is it Antares? Blast for Antares, believing it to be a planet, and you will never live to have grandchildren.&rdquo;</span>
            <nobr>
              &mdash;
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/quotes/103165-there-is-a-misconception-geocentric-and-anthropomorphic-common-to-the">6 likes</a>
            </nobr>
            <div class="clear"></div>
          </div>
        <a class="actionLink" style="float: right" href="/work/quotes/1021849-orphans-of-the-sky">More quotes&hellip;</a>
